Let's break down the key elements:

1. Exegesis: This term comes from the Greek word "exÄgÄsis," which means "interpretation" or "explanation." In Latin, it is commonly used to refer to the process of interpreting or explaining a text or concept.

2. Gnomon: The word "gnomon" is derived from the Greek word "gnÅmÅn," which means "indicator" or "interpreter." In Latin, it typically refers to the part of a sundial that casts a shadow, allowing one to determine the time of day.

When combined, the phrase "exegesis of the gnomon" suggests a scholarly analysis or interpretation of the gnomon, particularly in the context of understanding its purpose, function, or significance.

The gnomon, as a component of the sundial, holds significance not only as a practical tool for measuring time but also as a metaphor in literature and philosophy. For instance, Plato in his work "Republic" uses the analogy of the gnomon to describe the relationship between the world of forms and the world of appearances.Given this, the "exegesis of the gnomon" can refer to a detailed study of not just the sundial's physical component but also its metaphysical and philosophical implications.If you're delving into the topic, it would be intriguing to explore both the practical (how it functions, its historical evolution) and symbolic (its representation in literature, philosophy, or theology) aspects of the gnomon.Specifically I was referring to an explanation of the sundial, which could refer to the Bible in this case, and to have the the initials EG, a reference to Philip K. Dick's book and John A. Bengel's
Gnomon of the New Testament, 5 Volumes

 Bengel's work on the New Testament is a valuable resource for modern students of the Scriptures. In 1734, he published a carefully prepared Greek text of the New Testament with an 'Apparatus criticus', which formed the point of departure for modern New Testament textual criticism. His famous canon was: The more difficult reading is to be preferred. This critical work was followed by an exegetical one, 'Gnomon Novi Testamenti' (Tubingen, 1742). As a brief and suggestive commentary on the New Testament, the 'Gnomon' is still of considerable use today. Bengel's chief principle of interpretation, briefly stated, is to read nothing into the Scriptures, but to draw everything from them, and suffer nothing to remain hidden that is really in them. His 'Gnomon' exerted considerable influence on exegesis in Germany, and John Wesley translated most of its notes and incorporated them into his 'Annotatory Notes upon the New Testament' (London, 1755). A. Hauck, Schaff-Herzog Encyclopedia of Religious KnowledgeChatGPTUnderstood.
